24:850:60003:27:20:72:540:195:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:In the short-run, we have already discussed how an increase in demandLcand the corresponding
24:851:60095:20:21:72:539:175:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:increase  in  competitionλinduces  an  increase  in  the  cutoffs  ˆc=  ˆv.   In  this  scenario,  net  profit
24:852:60205:21:20:72:539:154:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:per-productLcπ(v,λ)−fis increasing for the high cost products with costvclose to the cutoff,
24:853:60297:20:20:72:539:134:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:even though the operating profitper-consumerπ(v,λ) is decreasing for all products (the increase
24:854:60392:20:17:72:540:114:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:in demandLcdominates the negative impact of the increase in competitionλ).  Condition (B1)
24:855:60482:17:15:80:539:97:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:28Bertoletti and Epifani (2014) derive a similar expression for the reallocation of revenue.  However, they do not
24:856:60596:15:0:72:407:82:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:show how that condition is implied by assumption (B1’) and requires (B1) to hold.
25:857:60679:0:21:72:539:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:then implies that the profits for the better performing products (with lower costv) increase dispro-
25:858:60779:21:20:71:539:688:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:portionately relative to the high cost products.  Thus, net operating profit per-product increases
25:859:60877:20:20:71:147:668:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:for all products.
25:860:60894:20:21:88:539:648:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:In the long-run, we mentioned that the change in the cutoffs in response to an increase in demand
25:861:60991:21:20:71:539:627:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:could not be determined without making additional assumptions on demand.  Under (B1), such a
25:862:61083:20:20:71:539:607:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:demand increase induces a disproportionate increase in operating profits for the best performing
25:863:61179:20:21:71:539:587:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:products;  thus,  total  firm  profit  Π(c,λ)  becomes  steeper  (as  a  function  of  firm  competencyc).
25:864:61285:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:The free entry condition (12) then requires a single crossing of the new steeper total firm profit
25:865:61383:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Π(c,λ) curve with the old flatter one (ensuring that average profit in both cases is still equal to the
25:866:61486:20:21:72:539:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:constant entry costfe).  This crossing defines a new profitability cutoff  ̃cwhereby better performing
25:867:61588:21:20:71:539:505:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:firms withc < ̃cenjoy a profit increase whereas worse performing firms withc > ̃csuffer a profit
25:868:61684:20:20:71:539:485:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:loss.  Hence, the zero profit cutoff ˆc(and the associated product-level cutoff ˆv) decreases, leading
25:869:61786:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:to the exit of the worse performing firms (and the worse performing products for all firms).  The
25:870:61883:21:20:72:539:444:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:product-level net-profit curveLcπ(v,λ)−f(as a function of product costv) rotates in a similar
25:871:61976:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:fashion to the firm-level profit curve:  profits for the best performing products increase while they
25:872:62077:20:21:72:539:404:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:decrease for the worse performing products.  For those high performing products with low costv,
25:873:62172:21:20:71:539:383:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the increase in demandLcdominates the effect of tougher competition (higherλ) on per-consumer
25:874:62265:20:20:71:432:363:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:profitsπ(v,λ); whereas the opposite holds for the low performing products.
25:875:62339:20:21:88:539:343:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We  further  note  that  (B1)  is  also  a  necessary  condition  for  this  selection  effect  in  the  long
25:876:62448:21:20:71:539:322:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:run:  If (B1) were violated then the profit curves would rotate in the opposite direction, reducing
25:877:62547:20:20:71:539:302:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:(increasing) profits for the best (worst) performing firms and products.  This would result in an
25:878:62644:20:21:71:540:282:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:increase  in  the  cutoffs  ˆc=  ˆv.   In  the  limiting  C.E.S.  case,  the  cutoffs  would  be  unaffected  by
25:879:62756:21:20:71:539:261:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:changes in demands:  the increase in demandLcis exactly offset by the increase in competition
25:880:62849:20:35:71:359:241:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:leaving net-profitsLcπ(v,λ)−funchanged for all products.
25:881:62905:35:28:71:183:206:11:g_d0_f10:11:g_d0_f10:4.2  Open Economy
25:882:62922:28:20:71:539:178:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:With  our  empirical  findings  in  mind,  we  consider  a  simplified  three-country  economy  consisting
25:883:63028:20:21:71:540:158:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of a Home country (H:  France) and a Foreign country (F:  Rest of World) both exporting to a
25:884:63120:21:20:72:539:137:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Destination country (D).  We focus on the equilibrium in this destinationD, characterized by a
25:885:63214:20:20:71:539:117:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:demand levelLcD.  For simplicity, we assume that this destination market is ‘small’ with respect to
25:886:63313:20:0:71:540:97:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:economy-wide outcomes inHandF, in the sense that changes in destinationD-specific variables do
26:887:63409:0:21:72:539:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:not affect those equilibrium outcomes (apart from those related to exports toD).29In particular,
26:888:63505:21:20:72:539:688:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:this entails that the number of entering firms inHandF,NeHandNeF, do not respond to changes in
26:889:63599:20:20:71:540:668:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:demandLcDinD.  (Naturally, the number of entrantsNeDintoDwill respond to demand conditions
26:890:63689:20:21:71:169:648:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:LcDin the long-run.)
26:891:63709:21:20:88:539:627:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Since  we  are  interested  in  highlighting  the  impact  of  demand  changes  in  destinationDfor
26:892:63808:20:20:71:539:607:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:competition there, we make one further simplification:  We assume that firms inDdo not export
26:893:63901:20:21:71:540:587:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:to eitherHorF.  In this case, trade is necessarily unbalanced so we restrict our analysis to the
26:894:63997:21:20:71:539:566:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:PE  case  and  assume  an  exogenous  unitary  wage.   In  the  appendix,  we  show  how  to  extend  the
26:895:64102:20:20:71:540:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:equilibrium conditions to the GE and PE cases where firms inDexport; and highlight how this
26:896:64193:20:21:71:516:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:would not change any of the qualitative results regarding the impact of demand shocks inD.
26:897:64283:21:20:88:539:505:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Exports  froml∈ {H,F}intoDincur  both  a  per-unit  iceberg  costτlD>1  as  well  as  a
26:898:64370:20:20:71:540:485:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:fixed export costflD.  In order to streamline the analysis of the production decisions for domestic
26:899:64469:20:21:71:539:465:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:producers selling inDalong with exporters intoD, we use the notationfDD< flDto denote the
26:900:64558:21:20:71:540:444:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:smaller fixed cost faced by domestic producers and introduceτDD≡1.  Lastly, we can allow for
26:901:64650:20:20:71:540:424:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:arbitrary technology differences across countriesl∈{H,F,D}in terms of the product ladder cost
26:902:64743:20:36:71:442:404:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:zl(m), the entry costfel, and the firm distribution of core competencies Γl(c).
26:903:64822:36:27:71:267:368:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f9:Product-Level Performance and Selection
26:904:64861:27:21:71:539:341:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We now consider the production decisions for sales intoD.  Since the marginal cost of production is
26:905:64960:21:20:71:539:320:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:constant and there are no cross-market or cross-product cost synergies, firms will make independent
26:906:65059:20:20:71:539:300:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:market-level decisions per-product.  Any productisold inDwill face the residual inverse demand
26:907:65153:20:21:71:539:280:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:(4) with a unique endogenous competition levelλDfor that market.  Any firm froml∈{H,F,D}
26:908:65241:21:20:71:540:259:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:owning the blueprint for productiwith marginal costvfaces a constant delivered marginal cost
26:909:65333:20:20:71:540:239:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:τlDvfor  marketD.   All  these  firms  therefore  solve  the  same  profit  maximization  problem  with
26:910:65436:20:21:71:539:219:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:respect to this delivered cost, associated with the same first order condition (6) as in the closed
26:911:65535:21:20:71:539:198:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:economy.  Thus, we can use the same optimal output, operating profit, and revenue functions as
26:912:65629:20:20:71:539:178:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:in  the  closed  economy  to  evaluate  the  performance  of  a  product  in  destinationD:q(τlDv,λD),
26:913:65731:20:21:72:539:158:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:π(τlDv,λD),r(τlDv,λD)  capture  those  performance  metrics  (per-consumer  inD)  for  a  product
26:914:65828:21:20:72:540:137:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:with  delivered  costτlDv.   Producers  froml∈ {H,F,D}will  sell  a  product  with  costvinDso
26:915:65922:20:16:71:539:117:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:long as the associated profits are non-negative.  This leads to three (for each origin) new zero-profit
26:916:66025:16:15:80:539:101:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:29This small open economy setup with monopolistic competition follows closely the development in Demidova and
26:917:66134:15:0:72:304:86:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:Rodrigues-Clare (2013) for the case of C.E.S. preferences.
27:918:66194:0:21:72:154:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:cutoff conditions:
27:919:66212:21:30:220:539:688:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:π(τlDˆvlD,λD) =flD, l∈{H,F,D}.(14)
27:920:66246:30:20:72:540:658:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:All products fromlwith costv≤ˆvlDwill be sold inD.  Since we have assumed that the fixed
27:921:66334:20:20:72:540:638:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:export cost is higher than the domestic market fixed cost (flD> fDDforl∈{H,F}), the marginal
27:922:66426:20:21:72:540:618:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:exported product will have a lower delivered cost than the marginal domestically produced product:
27:923:66524:21:20:72:539:597:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:τlDˆvlD<ˆvDD;  and  it  will  therefore  feature  strictly  higher  performance  measures  (in  terms  of
27:924:66629:20:20:72:261:577:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:operating profit, output, and revenues).
27:925:66669:20:21:88:539:557:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:These product-level cutoffs will also be equal to the firm-level cutoffs for the sale of any product
27:926:66769:21:20:72:539:536:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:inD:  Only  firms  with  core  competencyc≤ˆclD=  ˆvlDwill  operate  (sell)  in  marketD.   When
27:927:66865:20:20:72:539:516:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:l∈ {H,F},  those cutoffs represent the firm-level export cutoff intoD.  Whenl=D,  the cutoff
27:928:66957:20:36:72:392:496:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:represents the production (survival) cutoff for domestic firms inD.
27:929:67024:36:27:72:205:460:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f9:Free Entry in the Long Run
27:930:67050:27:21:72:539:433:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:The  free  entry  conditions  for  marketsHandFare  irrelevant  for  destinationD,  due  to  our  as-
27:931:67151:21:20:72:539:412:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f11:sumption of the former being ‘small’ relative to the latter two.  In the long run, free entry intoD
27:932:67250:20:20:72:540:392:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:equalizes the expected profits for prospective entrants with the sunk entry cost.  Thus, this new free
27:933:67352:20:21:72:539:372:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f11:entry condition is identical to the one for the closed economy; except for the labeling of country-D
27:934:67452:21:25:72:155:351:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:specific variables:

27:941:67516:0:18:411:417:331:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:]
27:942:67517:18:39:421:539:313:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:=feD.(15)
27:943:67526:39:21:72:540:274:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:As in the closed economy case, this free entry condition, along with the zero cutoff profit condition
27:944:67627:21:20:72:539:253:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:(14,  forl=D)  jointly  determine  the  equilibrium  cutoffs  ˆvDD=  ˆcDDalong  with  the  level  of

27:947:67880:21:20:88:539:192:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:In the open economy, consumers inDspread their income over all the domestically produced
27:948:67968:20:20:71:539:172:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:and imported products.  Since the number of entrants intoHandFare exogenous with respect to
27:949:68059:20:21:71:540:152:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:countryD, the budget constraint for consumers inDcan still be used to directly determine the
27:950:68151:21:29:71:457:131:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:endogenous number of entrants intoD(as was the case for the closed economy):

28:964:68292:0:28:72:180:709:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f9:Short-Run Equilibrium
28:965:68313:28:20:72:539:681:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:In the short-run, the number of incumbents inDis fixed at ̄ND(with the same exogenous distri-

28:967:68501:20:21:72:540:641:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:The budget constraint (16) still holds with the exogenous number of incumbents ̄NDreplacing the
28:968:68596:21:20:72:540:620:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:endogenous number of entrantsNeD.  Together with the three cutoff-profit conditions (14),  these
28:969:68692:20:20:72:540:600:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:four  equilibrium  conditions  determine  the  competition  levelλDalong  with  the  three  cutoffs  for
28:970:68796:20:36:72:138:580:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:l∈{H,F,D}.
28:971:68806:36:27:71:273:544:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f9:Demand Shocks and Product Reallocations
28:972:68845:27:21:71:539:517:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Using the same reasoning as previously developed for the closed economy case, we can show that
28:973:68939:21:20:71:539:496:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:an increase in demandLcDwill result in an increase in competitionλDinDfor both the long-run
28:974:69030:20:20:72:540:476:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:and short-run.30Thus, all of our previous results regarding the impact of such a demand shock
28:975:69123:20:21:72:540:456:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:on the reallocation of profits, output, and revenue towards better performing products still hold
28:976:69220:21:20:72:540:435:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:(Propositions 1-3).  Thus, for exporters toD, we can connect demand conditions (B1) and (B1’) to
28:977:69316:20:36:71:522:415:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the reallocation of export sales and profits (in marketD) towards better performing products.
28:978:69409:36:27:72:114:379:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f9:Selection
28:979:69418:27:20:72:540:352:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Again using a similar reasoning to our analysis of the closed economy case, demand condition (B1)
28:980:69515:20:21:72:540:332:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:is sufficient for the demand shock to increase the net profit for the sales of the best performing
28:981:69613:21:20:72:539:311:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:products inD(the profits generated by sales inD) in both the long-run and short-run.  Thus, so
28:982:69707:20:20:71:540:291:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:long as the fixed export costfHDis high enough, all exported products fromHwill fall into this
28:983:69801:20:21:71:539:271:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:category and experience a profit increase following the increase in demand.  This, in turn, implies
28:984:69900:21:20:71:539:250:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f11:that an increase in demand leads to a fall in the export cutoffs  ˆvHD:  Existing exporters fromH
28:985:69997:20:20:71:517:230:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:increase their range of exported products toD, and some firms fromHstart exporting toD.
28:986:70084:20:21:88:539:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:In the long-run, condition (B1) is also necessary for the demand shock to induce the selection
28:987:70178:21:20:72:540:189:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of newly exported products fromHtoD(so long as the fixed export costfHDis high enough).
28:988:70265:20:20:71:539:169:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Similar to the closed economy case, a violation of condition (B1) would imply a reduction in the
28:989:70361:20:21:71:539:149:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:profits of the best performing products toD, thus reversing the predicted consequences for export
28:990:70458:21:13:71:152:128:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:market selection.
28:991:70475:13:15:80:540:115:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:30Once again, a (weak) decrease in competitionλDwould necessarily lead to a violation of the free entry condition
28:992:70588:15:0:71:305:100:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:in the long-run, or the budget constraint in the short-run.
29:993:70649:0:21:72:520:709:11:g_d0_f10:11:g_d0_f10:4.3  Connecting Back to Empirical Measures of Product Reallocations in Export
29:994:70726:21:27:100:145:688:11:g_d0_f10:11:g_d0_f10:Markets
29:995:70733:27:20:72:540:661:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We have just shown how our open economy model with demand condition (B1’) can explain all
29:996:70822:20:21:72:540:641:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of  the  evidence  on  the  response  of  French  exporters  to  demand  shocks  that  we  documented  in
29:997:70927:21:20:72:540:620:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Section 3.  It explains how positive demand shocks induce the entry of new exported products and
29:998:71023:20:20:72:540:600:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the reallocation of output and revenues towards the best performing products.  The reallocation
29:999:71118:20:21:72:540:580:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of output contributes positively to a firm’s productivity (by shifting employment shares towards
29:1000:71214:21:20:72:539:559:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:products with higher marginal products).31And the reallocation of revenues generates an increase
29:1001:71310:20:20:71:540:539:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:in  the  skewness  of  a  firm’s  export  sales  to  that  destination.32Demand  condition  (B1’)  and  the
29:1002:71417:20:21:72:540:519:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:weaker version (B1) are also directly connected to the empirical evidence on firm/product markups
29:1003:71514:21:20:72:540:498:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:and  pass-through.   In  the  limiting  case  of  C.E.S.  preferences,  demand  shocks  in  export  market
29:1004:71620:20:20:72:540:478:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:would have no impact on the skewness of export sales; markups are constant across products; and
29:1005:71715:20:21:72:330:458:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:pass-through is complete (equal to 1) for all products.
29:1006:71770:21:20:88:539:437:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:On its own, the evidence on the positive relationship between demand shocks and export skew-
29:1007:71862:20:20:72:539:417:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:ness  requires  that  [1−εp(qi)]/εφ(qi)  is  decreasing  over  the  range  of  exported  outputqithat  we
29:1008:71967:20:21:72:539:397:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:observe.  We have pointed out that condition (B1’) (εφ(qi) increasing) is sufficient for this outcome.
29:1009:72069:21:20:72:540:376:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:However, since it is not a necessary condition, our evidence for the skewness of exports does not
29:1010:72166:20:20:72:540:356:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:imply that (B1’) must hold.  On the other hand, we show in the appendix that the weaker condition
29:1011:72263:20:21:72:539:336:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:(B1) (Marshall’s Second Law of Demand,εφ(qi) increasing) must nevertheless hold.  In particular,
29:1012:72359:21:20:72:539:315:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:we show that even if (B1) were violated over a portion of the relevant demand curve (εp(qi) de-
29:1013:72454:20:20:72:540:295:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:creasing over some range), then this would result in a reverse prediction for export skewness over
29:1014:72552:20:21:72:540:275:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:this portion of the demand curve.  This result also fits with what we know about the limiting case
29:1015:72650:21:20:72:513:254:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of C.E.S. preferences, where demand shocks have no impact on the skewness of export sales.
29:1016:72740:20:20:88:539:234:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Thus, our empirical evidence on the impact of demand shocks for export skewness provides an
29:1017:72831:20:21:72:540:214:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:independent confirmation for the empirical relevance of this critical property of demand – without
29:1018:72929:21:16:72:317:193:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:relying on the measurement of prices and markups.
29:1019:72978:16:11:80:516:177:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:31We document the strong empirical connection between demand shocks and firm productivity in Section 6.
29:1020:73081:11:14:80:539:166:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:32We  showed  how  the  ratio  of  export  sales  for  any  two  products  (with  the  better  performing  product  in  the
29:1021:73204:14:11:72:540:152:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:numerator)  increases  in  response  to  a  demand  shock.   This  clearly  increases  the  skewness  of  export  sales.   In  a
29:1022:73332:11:11:72:540:141:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:separate appendix, we confirm that such an increase in skewness is reflected in the Theil and Atkinson indices that
29:1023:73447:11:0:72:191:130:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:we use in our empirical work.
30:1024:73478:0:32:72:474:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:5  Trade Competition and Product Reallocations at the Firm-Level
30:1025:73542:32:20:72:540:677:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Our theoretical model highlights how our measured demand shocks induce increases in competi-
30:1026:73634:20:20:72:540:657:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:tion  for  exporters  to  those  destinations;  and  how  the  increased  competition  generates  increases
30:1027:73741:20:21:72:540:637:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:in  productivity  by  shifting  market  shares  and  employment  towards  better  performing  products.
30:1028:73844:21:20:72:540:616:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We seek to directly measure this connection between demand shocks and productivity.  Since we
30:1029:73937:20:20:72:540:596:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:cannot measure the productivity associated with products sold to a particular destination, we need
30:1030:74035:20:21:72:540:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:to show that the connection between demand shocks and product reallocations aggregates to the
30:1031:74128:21:20:72:540:555:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:firm-level – before examining the link with firm-level productivity changes (which we can directly
30:1032:74226:20:20:72:540:535:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:measure).  Our results in section 3 highlighted how demand shocks lead to reallocations towards
30:1033:74321:20:20:72:540:515:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:better  performing  products  at  the  destination-industry  level.   In  this  section,  we  show  how  the
30:1034:74429:20:21:72:540:495:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:destination-industry demand shocks can be aggregated to the firm-level – and this firm-level de-
30:1035:74525:21:20:72:540:474:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:mand  shock  strongly  predicts  product  reallocations  towards  better  performing  products  (higher
30:1036:74628:20:20:72:539:454:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:market shares) at thefirm-level; that is, changes in skewness to the firm’s global product mix (the
30:1037:74727:20:21:71:321:434:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:distribution of product sales across all destinations).
30:1038:74782:21:20:88:539:413:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Intuitively,  since  there  is  a  stable  ranking  of  products  at  the  firm  level  (better  performing
30:1039:74889:20:20:71:539:393:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:products in one market are most likely to be the better performing products in other markets – as
30:1040:74986:20:21:71:539:373:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:we previously discussed), then reallocations towards better performing products within destinations
30:1041:75085:21:20:71:539:352:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:should also be reflected in the reallocations of global sales/production towards better performing
30:1042:75183:20:20:71:539:332:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:products; and the strength of this link between the skewness of sales at the destination and global
30:1043:75282:20:21:71:539:312:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:levels should depend on the importance of the destination in the firm’s global sales.  Our chosen
30:1044:75379:21:20:71:539:291:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:measure  of  skewness,  the  Theil  index,  makes  this  intuition  precise.   It  is  the  only  measure  of
30:1045:75488:20:20:71:539:271:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:skewness that exhibits a stable decomposition from the skewness of global sales into the skewness
30:1046:75585:20:21:71:539:251:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of  destination-level  sales  (see  Jost  2007).33Specifically,  letTi,tbe  firmi’s  Theil  index  for  the
30:1047:75692:21:0:71:308:230:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:skewness  of  its  global  exports  by  productxsi,t≡
30:1048:75745:0:11:313:325:238:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:∑
30:1049:75746:11:0:325:337:227:8:g_d0_f12:11:g_d0_f11:dx
30:1050:75748:0:7:337:341:234:8:g_d0_f12:8:g_d0_f12:s
30:1051:75749:7:17:337:540:227:8:g_d0_f12:11:g_d0_f4:i,d,t(the  sum  of  exports  for  that  product
30:1052:75796:17:20:71:540:210:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:across all destinations).34Then this global Theil can be decomposed into a market-share weighted
30:1053:75892:20:19:71:540:190:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:average of the within-destination TheilsTi,d,tand a “between-destination” Theil indexTBi,d,tthat
30:1054:75988:19:15:80:539:171:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:33This decomposition property is similar – but not identical – to the within/between decomposition of Theil indices
30:1055:76103:15:11:72:540:156:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:across populations.  In the latter, the sample is split into subsamples.  In our case, the same observation (in this case,
30:1056:76225:11:7:72:492:145:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:product sales) is split into “destinations” and the global measure reflects the sum across “destinations”.
30:1057:76331:7:15:80:539:138:6:g_d0_f7:6:g_d0_f15:34The Theil index is defined in the same way as the destination level Theil in (2).  We return to our notationxsi,d,t
30:1058:76448:15:11:71:539:123:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:to denote firmi’s exports of productsto destinationdin yeart, which we use throughout our empirical analysis.
30:1059:76557:11:11:71:540:112:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:In terms of our theoretical model, this corresponds to the export revenues generated for a productmby a firm with
30:1060:76670:11:0:71:219:101:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:core competencycto destinationD.
31:1061:76704:0:37:72:511:709:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f6:measures differences in the distribution of product-level market shares across destinations:35
31:1062:76798:37:0:219:245:672:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:Ti,t=
31:1063:76803:0:24:249:265:682:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:∑
31:1064:76804:24:0:254:258:658:8:g_d0_f12:8:g_d0_f12:d
31:1065:76805:0:15:267:289:679:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:xi,d,t
31:1066:76811:15:0:271:285:664:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:xi,t
31:1067:76815:0:0:290:323:672:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f5:Ti,d,t−

31:1071:76830:15:0:348:362:664:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:xi,t
31:1072:76834:0:40:367:539:672:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:TBi,d,t,(17)
31:1073:76846:40:0:72:137:632:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:wherexi,d,t≡
31:1074:76858:0:12:141:153:640:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:∑

31:1078:76875:0:12:237:249:640:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:∑

31:1080:76937:17:41:72:489:611:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:nations to the world (global exports).  The between-destination TheilTBi,d,tis defined as
31:1081:77026:41:0:221:254:570:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:TBi,d,t=
31:1082:77034:0:23:257:273:580:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:∑
31:1083:77035:23:0:263:267:557:8:g_d0_f12:8:g_d0_f12:s
31:1084:77036:0:16:276:297:579:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:xsi,d,t
31:1085:77043:16:0:276:297:563:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:xi,d,t
31:1086:77049:0:0:301:315:570:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:log
31:1087:77052:0:10:317:326:589:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:(
31:1088:77053:10:16:327:375:579:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:xsi,d,t/xi,d,t
31:1089:77067:16:0:333:368:563:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:xsi,t/xi,t
31:1090:77077:0:19:377:386:589:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:)
31:1091:77078:19:39:387:390:570:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f11:.
31:1092:77079:39:20:72:540:531:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Note  that  the  weights  used  in  this  decomposition  for  both  the  within-  and  between-destination
31:1093:77185:20:20:72:539:511:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Theils are the firm’s export sharesxi,d,t/xi,tacross destinationsd.  The between-destination Theil
31:1094:77283:20:21:72:540:491:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:TBi,d,tmeasures the deviation in a product’s market share in a destinationd,xsi,d,t/xi,d,t, from that
31:1095:77384:21:20:71:539:470:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:product’s global market sharexsi,t/xi,tand then averages these deviations across destinations.  It is
31:1096:77485:20:20:71:539:450:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:positive and converges to zero as the distributions of product market shares in different destination
31:1097:77586:20:21:71:205:430:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:become increasingly similar.
31:1098:77614:21:20:88:539:409:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:To better understand the logic behind (17), note that it implies that the average of the within-
31:1099:77710:20:20:71:539:389:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:destination  Theil  indices  can  be  decomposed  into  the  sum  of  two  positive  elements:  the  global
31:1100:77817:20:21:71:539:369:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Theil index, and the between-destination Theil index.  This decomposition can be interpreted as
31:1101:77912:21:20:71:539:348:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:a decomposition of variance/dispersion.  The dispersion observed in the destination level product
31:1102:78009:20:20:71:539:328:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:exports must be explained either by dispersion in global product exports (global Theil index), or
31:1103:78106:20:21:71:539:308:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:by the fact that the distribution of product sales varies across destinations (between-destination
31:1104:78204:21:20:71:133:287:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Theil index).
31:1105:78217:20:20:88:539:267:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:A simple example helps to clarify this point.  Take a firm with 2 products and 2 destinations.
31:1106:78311:20:21:71:539:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:In each destination, exports of one product arex, and exports of the other product are 2x.  This
31:1107:78407:21:20:72:540:226:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:leads to the same value for the within-destination Theil indices of (1/3) ln (1/3) + (2/3) ln(2/3),
31:1108:78506:20:20:72:540:206:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:and  hence  the  same  value  for  the  average  within-destination  Theil  index.   Hence,  if  the  same
31:1109:78612:20:20:72:540:186:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:product is the better performing product in each market (with 2xexports), then the distributions
31:1110:78708:20:21:72:540:166:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:will be synchronized across destinations and the between-destination Theil will be zero:  all of the
31:1111:78808:21:20:72:540:145:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:dispersion is explained by the global Theil index, whose value is equal to the common value of the
31:1112:78906:20:20:72:540:125:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:two within-destination Theil indices.  On the other hand, if the opposite products perform better
31:1113:79003:20:16:72:539:105:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:in each market, global sales are 3xfor each product.  There is thus no variation in global product
31:1114:79101:16:15:80:539:89:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:35For simplicity, we omit the industry referencingIfor the destination Theils.  The decomposition across industries
31:1115:79216:15:0:72:172:74:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:follows a similar pattern.
32:1116:79244:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:sales, and the global Theil index is zero.  Accordingly, all of the variation in the within-destination
32:1117:79347:21:20:72:356:688:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Theil indices is explained by the between-destination Theil.
32:1118:79407:20:20:88:539:668:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:The theoretical model of Bernard, Redding and Schott (2011) with CES demand predicts that
32:1119:79496:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the  between-destination  Theil  index  would  be  exactly  zero  when  measured  on  a  common  set  of
32:1120:79600:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:exported products across destinations.  With linear demand, Mayer, Melitz and Ottaviano (2014)
32:1121:79694:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:show (theoretically and empirically) that this between-destination index would deviate from zero
32:1122:79790:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:because  skewness  varies  across  destinations.   We  have  shown  earlier  that  this  result  holds  for
32:1123:79897:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:a  larger  class  of  demand  systems  such  that  the  elasticity  and  the  convexity  of  inverse  demand
32:1124:80005:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:increase with consumption.  Yet, even in these cases, the between-destination Theil is predicted to
32:1125:80104:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:be small because the ranking of the product sales is very stable across destinations.  This leads to
32:1126:80204:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:a prediction that the market-share weighted average of the destination Theils should be strongly
32:1127:80300:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:correlated with the firm’s global Theil.  Empirically, this prediction is strongly confirmed as shown
32:1128:80401:20:244:72:349:465:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:in the firm-level scatter plot (across all years) in Figure 2.
32:1129:80463:244:32:121:489:221:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Figure 2:  Correlation Between Global Skewness and Average Local Skewness
32:1130:80536:32:20:88:539:189:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:This  high  correlation  between  destination  and  global  skewness  of  product  sales  enables  us
32:1131:80637:20:20:72:540:169:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:to  move  from  our  previous  predictions  for  the  effects  of  the  demand  shocks  on  skewness  at  the
32:1132:80746:20:21:72:540:149:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:destination-level to a new prediction at the firm-level.  To do this, we aggregate our destination-
32:1133:80845:21:20:72:540:128:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:industry measures of demand shocks to the firm-level using the same weighing scheme by the firms’
32:1134:80942:20:0:72:540:108:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:export shares across destinations.  We thus obtain our firm-level demand shock in (log) levels and
33:1135:81042:0:37:72:143:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:first difference:

33:1139:81072:0:17:196:221:681:11:g_d0_f11:6:g_d0_f7:xIi,d,t0

33:1151:81161:7:17:163:539:626:8:g_d0_f12:11:g_d0_f4:i,d,trepresents firmi’s total exports in yeart.  As was the case for the construc-
33:1152:81243:17:21:72:540:609:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:tion of our firm-level destination shock (see equation 1), we only use the firm-level information on
33:1153:81343:21:20:72:539:588:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:exported products and market shares in prior years (the year of first export salest0for the demand
33:1154:81441:20:20:72:540:568:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:shock in levels and lagged yeart−1 for the  first difference betweentandt−1).  This  ensures
33:1155:81533:20:21:72:540:548:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the exogeneity of our constructed firm-level demand shocks (exogenous to firm-level actions in year
33:1156:81632:21:20:72:539:527:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:t > t0for levels, and exogenous to firm-level changes ∆tfor first differences).  In particular, changes
33:1157:81735:20:20:72:539:507:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f6:in the set of exported products or exported market shares are not reflected in the demand shock.36
33:1158:81833:20:21:88:539:487:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:By construction, our firm-level demand shocks will predict changes in the weighted average of
33:1159:81926:21:20:72:539:466:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:destination skewnessTi,d,t– and hence will predict changes in the firm’s global skewnessTi,t(given
33:1160:82024:20:20:72:540:446:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the high correlation between the two indices).  This result is confirmed by our regression of the
33:1161:82121:20:21:72:540:426:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:firms’ global Theil on our trade shock measures, reported in the first three columns of Table 6.  Our
33:1162:82222:21:20:72:540:405:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:firm-level trade shock has a strong and highly significant (again, well beyond the 1% significance
33:1163:82320:20:20:72:540:385:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:level) impact on the skewness of global exports.  The industry level trade shock – which was already
33:1164:82420:20:21:72:540:365:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:substantially weaker than the firm-level trade shock in the destination-level regressions – is no longer
33:1165:82524:21:20:72:540:344:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:significant at the firm level.  The GDP shock is not significant in the (log) levels regressions, but is
33:1166:82628:20:20:72:392:324:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:very strong and significant in the two first-difference specifications.
33:1167:82699:20:21:88:540:304:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Our  global  Theil  measureTi,tmeasures  the  skewness  of  export  sales  across  all  destinations,
33:1168:82800:21:20:72:540:283:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:but it does not entirely reflect the skewness of production levels across the firm’s product range.
33:1169:82899:20:20:72:540:263:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:That is because we cannot measure the breakdown of product-level sales on the French domestic
33:1170:82992:20:21:72:540:243:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:market.   Ultimately,  it  is  the  distribution  of  labor  allocation  across  products  (and  the  induced
33:1171:83101:21:20:72:540:222:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:distribution of production levels) that determines a firm’s labor productivity – conditional on its
33:1172:83200:20:20:72:540:202:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:technology (the production functions for each individual product). As highlighted by our theoretical
33:1173:83300:20:21:72:540:182:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:model, the export market demand shocks generate two different types of reallocations that both
33:1174:83394:21:20:72:540:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:contribute to an increased skewness of production levels for the firm:  reallocations within the set of
33:1175:83497:20:20:72:540:141:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:exported products, which generate the increased skewness of global exports that we just discussed;
33:1176:83595:20:16:72:540:121:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:but also reallocations from non-exported products towards the better performing exported products
33:1177:83692:16:15:80:539:105:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:36Lileeva and Trefler (2010), Hummels et al (2014), and Bernard et al (2014) use a similar strategy to construct
33:1178:83804:15:0:72:220:90:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:firm-level trade-related trade shocks.

34:1199:84725:20:20:72:540:327:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:statistic  that  reflects  this  reallocation  from  non-exported  to  exported  goods:   the  firm’s  export
34:1200:84834:20:21:72:540:307:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:intensity.  We can thus test whether the demand shocks also induce an increase in the firm’s export
34:1201:84933:21:20:72:540:286:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:intensity.  Those regressions are reported in the last three columns of Table 6,  and confirm that
34:1202:85031:20:20:72:540:266:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:our  firm-level  trade  shock  has  a  very  strong  and  highly  significant  positive  impact  on  a  firm’s
34:1203:85141:20:21:72:539:246:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:export intensity.37The impact of the GDP coefficient is also strong and significant, whereas the
34:1204:85237:21:20:72:540:225:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:industry-level trade shock remains insignificant.  Thus, our firm-level trade shock and GDP shock
34:1205:85334:20:20:72:540:205:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:both predict the two types of reallocations towards better performing products that we highlighted
34:1206:85432:20:17:72:475:185:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:in our theoretical model (as a response to increased competition in export markets).
34:1207:85516:17:15:80:539:168:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:37Since the export intensity is a ratio, we do not apply a log-transformation to that variable. However, specifications
34:1208:85635:15:0:72:306:153:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:using the log of export intensity yield very similar results.
35:1209:85698:0:32:72:309:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:6  Trade Competition and Productivity
35:1210:85735:32:20:72:540:677:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We  just  showed  that  our  firm-level  measure  of  demand  shocks  (aggregated  across  destinations)
35:1211:85839:20:20:72:540:657:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:predicts  increases  in  the  skewness  of  global  exports,  and  increases  in  export  intensity.   Holding
35:1212:85949:20:21:72:540:637:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:firm technology fixed (the productivity of each individual product), this increase in the skewness
35:1213:86047:21:20:72:540:616:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of global production will generate productivity increases for the firm.  We now directly test for this
35:1214:86149:20:20:72:344:596:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:connection from the demand shocks to firm productivity.
35:1215:86204:20:21:88:539:576:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We obtain our measure of firm productivity by merging our firm-level trade data with firm-level
35:1216:86299:21:20:72:540:555:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:production  data.   This  latter  dataset  contains  various  measures  of  firm  outputs  and  inputs.   As
35:1217:86407:20:20:72:540:535:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:we are interested in picking up productivity fluctuations at a yearly frequency, we focus on labor
35:1218:86505:20:20:72:540:515:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:productivity.  We then separately control for the impact of changes in factor intensities and returns
35:1219:86606:20:21:72:371:495:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:to scale (or variable utilization of labor) on labor productivity.
35:1220:86672:21:20:88:539:474:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We compute labor productivity at the firm-level as deflated value added per worker assuming
35:1221:86763:20:20:72:540:454:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:a sector-specific price deflatorPI.  Note that this measure aggregates to the overall deflated value-
35:1222:86864:20:21:72:540:434:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:added  per  worker  for  manufacturing.   This  aggregate  productivity  measure  accurately  tracks  a
35:1223:86967:21:20:72:540:413:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:welfare-relevant quantity index – even though we do not have access to firm-level prices:  The effect
35:1224:87068:20:20:72:506:393:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f6:of pure markup changes at the industry level are netted-out of our productivity measure.38
35:1225:87158:20:21:88:539:373:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:More formally,  we can write the welfare-relevant aggregate industry labor productivity – the
35:1226:87251:21:20:72:539:352:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:ratio of industry deflated value-added (VAI/PI) over industry employmentLI– as the labor share
35:1227:87345:20:37:71:540:332:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:weighted average of firm productivity using that same industry price deflatorPIfor firm revenue:
35:1228:87441:37:0:228:252:295:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f5:ΦI≡
35:1229:87444:0:15:257:294:303:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f12:VAI/PI
35:1230:87450:15:0:270:281:288:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:LI
35:1231:87452:0:0:299:307:295:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:=
35:1232:87453:0:24:311:327:306:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:∑
35:1233:87454:24:0:312:325:282:8:g_d0_f12:8:g_d0_f12:i∈I
35:1234:87457:0:15:330:341:303:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:Li
35:1235:87459:15:0:330:341:288:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:LI
35:1236:87461:0:15:344:381:303:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f12:VAi/PI
35:1237:87467:15:0:358:368:288:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f12:Li
35:1238:87469:0:42:520:539:295:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:(18)
35:1239:87473:42:0:72:372:253:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:In  other  words,  the  revenue-based  firm  productivity  measure
35:1240:87539:0:9:377:382:262:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:(
35:1241:87540:9:0:382:418:253:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f12:VAi/PI
35:1242:87546:0:9:419:424:262:11:g_d0_f16:11:g_d0_f16:)
35:1243:87547:9:20:426:539:253:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:/Liaggregates  up  to  a
35:1244:87571:20:20:71:540:233:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:quantity-based industry measure, using the empirically observed firm labor sharesLi/LIand with-
35:1245:87666:20:21:71:539:213:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:out any need for a quantity based output or productivity measure at the level of the firm (which
35:1246:87762:21:20:71:539:192:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:would require measures of firm-product-level prices and qualities).  This implicitly assumes the exis-
35:1247:87864:20:20:71:539:172:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:tence of an industry price aggregatorPI, though its functional form is left completely unrestricted.
35:1248:87964:20:20:71:539:152:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Consequently, we run all of our specifications with sector-time (2 digit NACE) fixed effects, thus
35:1249:88062:20:16:71:539:132:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:eliminating  the  need  for  any  direct  measures  of  those  sector-level  deflators.39Our  productivity
35:1250:88168:16:15:80:539:116:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:38At the firm-level, an increase in markups across all products will be picked-up in our firm productivity measure
35:1251:88282:15:11:72:540:101:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:– even though this does not reflect a welfare-relevant increase in output.  But if this is the case, then this firm’s labor
35:1252:88405:11:7:72:438:90:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:share will decrease, and its productivity will carry a smaller weight in the aggregate index.
35:1253:88498:7:0:80:539:83:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:39We have also experimented with an alternative procedure using deflated value-added per worker (the value added
36:1254:88612:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:results therefore capture within-sector effects of the demand shocks, over-and-above any contribu-
36:1255:88710:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:tion of the sector deflator to a common productivity change across firms.  We will thus report a
36:1256:88806:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:welfare-relevant aggregate productivity change by aggregating our firm-level productivity changes
36:1257:88903:20:21:72:278:648:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:using the observed changes in labor shares.
36:1258:88946:21:20:88:539:627:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Our  firm-level  demand  shocks  only  aggregate  across  export  destinations.   They  therefore  do
36:1259:89047:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:not incorporate a firm’s exposure to demand shocks in its domestic (French) market.  This is not
36:1260:89143:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:possible  for  two  reasons:   most  importantly,  we  do  not  observe  the  product-level  breakdown  of
36:1261:89249:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the  firms’  sales  in  the  French  market  (we  only  observe  total  domestic  sales  across  products);  in
36:1262:89360:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:addition,  world  exports  into  France  would  not  be  exogenous  to  firm-level  technology  changes  in
36:1263:89467:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:France.   Therefore,  we  need  to  adjust  our  export-specific  demand  shock  using  the  firm’s  export
36:1264:89574:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:intensity to obtain an overall firm-level demand shock relevant for overall production and hence
36:1265:89670:20:37:72:134:485:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:productivity:
36:1266:89683:37:0:96:174:448:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:shockintensi,t=
36:1267:89698:0:15:199:218:456:11:g_d0_f11:6:g_d0_f7:xi,t0
36:1268:89703:15:0:179:238:441:11:g_d0_f11:6:g_d0_f7:xi,t0+xi,F,t0
36:1269:89716:0:0:240:389:448:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:shocki,t, ̃∆shockintensi,t=
36:1270:89743:0:15:417:442:456:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f6:xi,t−1
36:1271:89749:15:0:394:466:441:11:g_d0_f11:8:g_d0_f6:xi,t−1+xi,F,t−1
36:1272:89764:0:40:470:515:451:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f11: ̃∆shocki,t,
36:1273:89776:40:20:71:539:411:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:wherexi,F,tdenotes firmi’s total (across products) sales to the French domestic market in year
36:1274:89870:20:20:71:539:391:11:g_d0_f11:11:g_d0_f4:t(and the ratio thus measures firmi’s export intensity).40Once again, we only use prior year’s
36:1275:89964:20:21:72:540:371:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:information on firm-level sales to construct this overall demand shock.  Note that this adjustment
36:1276:90062:21:20:72:540:350:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:using export intensity is equivalent to assuming a demand shock of zero in the French market and
36:1277:90158:20:35:72:532:330:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:including that market in our aggregation by market share relative to total firm salesxi,t+xi,F,t.
36:1278:90255:35:28:72:370:295:11:g_d0_f10:11:g_d0_f10:6.1  Impact of the Trade Shock on Firm Productivity
36:1279:90306:28:20:72:540:267:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:In this section, we investigate the direct link between this firm-level demand shock and firm produc-
36:1280:90407:20:20:72:540:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:tivity.  Here, we focus exclusively on our firm-specific demand shock from (1).  Most importantly,
36:1281:90505:20:21:72:539:227:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:this is our only demand shock that exhibits variation across firms within destinations.41In addition,
36:1282:90606:21:20:72:540:206:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:this is the demand shock that induced the strongest and most robust response in the skewness of
36:1283:90701:20:20:72:540:186:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the intensive margin of product exports.  Our measure of productivity is the log of value-added per
36:1284:90800:20:11:72:540:166:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:deflator  coming  from  EUKLEMS  dataset  for  France)  and  year  fixed-effects  (dropping  the  industry-year  combined
36:1285:90921:11:7:72:305:155:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:fixed effects).  The differences in the results are negligible.
36:1286:90984:7:15:80:540:148:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:40Sincexi,F,tis only available in the firm balance sheet data (and not in the customs data), we use the reported firm
36:1287:91101:15:11:72:540:133:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:total export figurexi,tfrom the same balance sheet data to compute this ratio. This ensures a consistent measurement
36:1288:91217:11:11:72:540:122:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:for the firm’s export intensity.  The correlation between this firm total export figure and the one reported by customs
36:1289:91336:11:7:72:410:111:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:is .95 (very high, though exhibiting some differences between the two data sources).
36:1290:91420:7:15:80:539:104:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:41As a robustness check reported in appendix C, we purge the trade shock of any industry-destination-year effect
36:1291:91532:15:11:72:540:89:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:in order to eliminate any correlation between destination characteristics and unobserved firm attributes associated
36:1292:91647:11:0:72:486:78:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:with higher productivity growth.  This approach precludes using the GDP and ISIC level trade shocks.
37:1293:91749:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:worker.  All regressions include industry-year fixed effects, that will capture in particular different
37:1294:91852:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:evolutions of price indexes across industries.  In order to control for changes in capital intensity,
37:1295:91953:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:we use the log of capital per worker.  We also control for unobserved changes in labor utilization
37:1296:92051:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:and returns to scale by using the log of raw materials (including energy use).  Then, increases in
37:1297:92149:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:worker effort or higher returns to scale will be reflected in the impact of raw materials use on labor
37:1298:92251:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:productivity.  As there is no issue with zeros for all these firm-level variables, we directly measure
37:1299:92353:20:21:72:447:587:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the growth rate of those variable using simple first differences of the log levels.
37:1300:92436:21:20:88:539:566:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We  begin  with  a  graphical  representation  of  the  strong  positive  relationship  between  firm-
37:1301:92538:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:level productivity and our constructed demand shock.  Figure 3 illustrates the correlation between
37:1302:92636:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:those  variables  in  first  differences  for  the  largest  French  exporters  (representing  50%  of  French
37:1303:92746:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:exports in 1996).  Panel (a) is the unconditional scatter plot for those variables, while panel (b)
37:1304:92845:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:shows the added-variable plot for the first-difference regression of productivity on the trade shock,
37:1305:92946:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:with additional controls for capital intensity, raw materials (both in log first-differences) and time
37:1306:93048:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:dummies.  Those figures clearly highlight the very strong positive response of the large exporters’
37:1307:93147:20:33:72:537:424:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:productivity to changes in trade competition in export markets (captured by the demand shock).
37:1308:93241:33:179:86:524:391:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Figure 3:  Exporters Representing 50% of French Trade in 1996:  First Differences 1996-2005
37:1309:93378:179:9:113:287:212:56:Helvetica:56:Helvetica:-.2-.10.1.2
37:1310:93389:9:9:159:247:203:56:Helvetica:56:Helvetica: change in log trade shock
37:1311:93415:9:12:114:280:194:36:Helvetica:36:Helvetica:regression line: coef =  .614, se =  .174, N = 977sample: 168 firms representing 50% of French exports in 1996
37:1312:93525:12:0:114:200:182:36:Helvetica:36:Helvetica:standard errors clustered by firm
37:1313:93610:0:9:354:518:212:56:Helvetica:56:Helvetica:-.2-.10.1.2
37:1314:93621:9:9:379:481:203:56:Helvetica:56:Helvetica:cond. change in ln trade shock
37:1315:93651:9:12:342:508:194:36:Helvetica:36:Helvetica:regression line: coef =  .756, se =  .23, N = 974sample: 168 firms representing 50% of French exports in 1996
37:1316:93760:12:23:342:428:182:36:Helvetica:36:Helvetica:standard errors clustered by firm
37:1317:93793:23:34:149:457:159:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:(a) Unconditional(b) Conditional
37:1318:93825:34:20:88:539:125:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Table  7  shows  how  this  result  generalizes  to  our  full  sample  of  firms  and  our  three  different
37:1319:93934:20:21:72:540:105:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:specifications  (FE,  FD,  FD-FE).  Our  theoretical  model  emphasizes  how  a  multi-product  firm’s
37:1320:94036:21:0:72:540:84:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:productivity responds to the demand shock via its effect on competition and product reallocations
38:1321:94135:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:in the firm’s export markets.  Thus, we assumed that the firm’s technology at the product level (the
38:1322:94235:21:20:72:539:688:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:marginal costv(m,c) for each productm) was exogenous (in particular, with respect to demand
38:1323:94326:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:fluctuations  in  export  markets).   However,  there  is  a  substantial  literature  examining  how  this
38:1324:94433:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:technology  responds  to  export  market  conditions  via  various  forms  of  innovation  or  investment
38:1325:94538:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:choices made by the firm.  We feel that the timing dimension of our first difference specifications –
38:1326:94639:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:especially our FD-FE specifications which nets out any firm-level growth trends – eliminates this
38:1327:94736:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:technology response channel:  It is highly unlikely that a firm’s innovation or investment response
38:1328:94835:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:to the trade shock in a given year (especially with respect to the trade shock’s deviation from trend
38:1329:94936:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:growth in the FD-FE specification) would be reflected contemporaneously in the firm’s productivity.
38:1330:95035:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:However, we will also show some additional robustness checks that address this potential technology
38:1331:95134:21:20:72:115:505:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:response.
38:1332:95143:20:20:88:539:485:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:The first three columns of Table 7 show that,  across our three timing specifications,  there is
38:1333:95239:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:a  stable  and  very  strong  response  of  firm  productivity  to  the  trade  shock.   Since  our  measure
38:1334:95347:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of  productivity  as  value  added  per  worker  incorporates  neither  the  impact  of  changes  in  input
38:1335:95454:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:intensities  nor  the  effects  of  non-constant  returns  to  scale,  we  directly  control  for  these  effects
38:1336:95567:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:in the next set of regressions.  In the last 3 columns of Table 7,  we add controls for capital per
38:1337:95666:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:worker and raw material use (including energy).  Both of these controls are highly significant:  not
38:1338:95766:20:20:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:surprisingly,  increases  in  capital  intensity  are  reflected  in  labor  productivity;  and  we  find  that
38:1339:95877:20:21:72:540:343:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:increases in raw materials use are also associated with higher labor productivity.  This would be
38:1340:95974:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the case if there are increasing returns to scale in the value-added production function, or if labor
38:1341:96075:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:utilization/effort increases with scale (in the short-run).  However,  even when these controls are
38:1342:96174:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:added,  the  very  strong  effect  of  the  trade  shock  on  firm  productivity  remains  highly  significant
38:1343:96284:21:20:72:540:261:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:well beyond the 1% significance level (from here on out, we will keep those controls in all of our
38:1344:96382:20:20:72:241:241:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:firm-level productivity regressions).
38:1345:96419:20:21:88:539:221:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Although  our  trade  shock  variable  exhibits  variation  within  destinations  it  nevertheless  also
38:1346:96523:21:20:72:540:200:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:reflects  the  impact  of  aggregate  destination  shocks.   This  could  potentially  generate  a  selection
38:1347:96632:20:20:72:540:180:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:bias if firms with unobserved characteristics leading to higher productivity growth self-select into
38:1348:96732:20:21:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:destinations with positive aggregate destination shocks (or vice-versa).  In order to eliminate this
38:1349:96832:21:20:72:540:139:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:possibility, we generate a version of our trade shock that is purged (demeaned) of any industry-
38:1350:96928:20:20:72:540:119:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:destination-year effects (trends for the FD-FE specification).  Using this purged measure instead of
38:1351:97028:20:20:72:540:99:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:our original one strengthen the impact of the trade shock on firm productivity (but leaves the impact
38:1352:97129:20:0:72:524:79:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of the other controls virtually un-changed).  This robustness check is reported in the appendix.

39:1371:97938:34:20:88:539:369:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We now describe several robustness checks that further single-out our theoretical mechanism
39:1372:98029:20:20:71:539:349:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:operating through the demand-side product reallocations for multi-product firms.  In the next table
39:1373:98128:20:21:71:539:329:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:we regress our capital intensity measure on our trade shock; the results in Table 8 show that there
39:1374:98227:21:20:71:539:308:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:is  no  response  of  investment  to  the  trade  shock.   This  represents  another  way  to  show  that  the
39:1375:98337:20:20:71:539:288:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:short-run timing for the demand shocks precludes a contemporaneous technology response:  if this
39:1376:98433:20:21:71:539:268:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:were the case, we would expect to see some of this response reflected in higher investment (along
39:1377:98530:21:20:71:330:247:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:with other responses along the technology dimension).
39:1378:98583:20:20:88:539:227:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Next  we  use  a  different  strategy  to  control  for  the  effects  of  non-constant  returns  to  scale
39:1379:98690:20:21:71:539:207:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:or variable labor utilization:  in Table 9,  we split our sample between year intervals where firms
39:1380:98789:21:20:71:539:186:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:increase/decrease employment.  If the effects of the trade shock on productivity were driven by scale
39:1381:98890:20:20:71:539:166:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:effects or higher labor utilization/effort,  then we would expect to see the productivity responses
39:1382:98989:20:20:71:539:146:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:concentrated in the split of the sample where firms are expanding employment (and also expanding
39:1383:99085:20:21:71:539:126:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:more  generally).   Yet,  Table  9  shows  that  this  is  not  the  case:  the  effect  of  the  trade  shock  on
39:1384:99199:21:20:71:539:105:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:productivity  is  just  as  strong  (even  a  bit  stronger)  in  the  sub-sample  of  years  where  firms  are
39:1385:99310:20:0:71:539:85:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:decreasing employment; and in both cases, the coefficients have a similar magnitude to our baseline

41:1443:102055:32:21:88:539:269:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:In  order  to  further  single-out  our  theoretical  mechanism  operating  through  the  demand-side
41:1444:102156:21:20:71:539:248:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:product  reallocations  for  multi-product  firms,  we  now  report  two  different  types  of  falsification
41:1445:102265:20:20:71:539:228:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:tests.   Our  first  test  highlights  that  the  link  between  productivity  and  the  trade  shocks  is  only
41:1446:102377:20:21:71:539:208:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:operative  for  multi-product  firms.   Table  11  reports  the  same  regression  (with  controls)  as  our
41:1447:102485:21:20:71:539:187:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:baseline results from Table 7, but only for single-product exporters.  This new table clearly shows
41:1448:102584:20:20:71:539:167:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:that  this  there  is  no  evidence  of  this  link  among  this  subset  of  firms.   Next,  we  show  that  this
41:1449:102698:20:21:71:539:147:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:productivity-trade link is only operative for firms with a substantial exposure to export markets
41:1450:102795:21:20:71:539:126:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:(measured by export intensity).  Similarly to single-product firms, we would not expect to find a
41:1451:102892:20:20:71:539:106:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:significant productivity-trade link among firms with very low export intensity.  This is indeed the
41:1452:102991:20:0:71:539:86:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:case.  In Table 12, we re-run our baseline specification using the trade shock before it is interacted
42:1453:103095:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:with export intensity.  The first three columns report the results for the quartile of firms with the
42:1454:103196:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:lowest export intensity, and highlight that there is no evidence of the productivity-trade link for
42:1455:103295:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:those firms.  On the other hand, we clearly see from the last three columns that this effect is very
42:1456:103395:20:21:72:453:648:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f6:strong and powerful for the quartile of firms with the highest export intensity.43
42:1457:103477:21:20:88:539:627:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:When we focus on this top quartile for export intensity, we are restricting our sample to sub-
42:1458:103571:20:20:71:539:607:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:stantially larger exporters.  If a French exporter is dominant in some of its export destinations, then
42:1459:103674:20:21:71:539:587:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:our exogeneity assumption for rest-of-world exports to those destinations may be violated.  In this
42:1460:103773:21:20:71:539:566:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:situation where a French firm has a substantial market share for some destination-product market,
42:1461:103870:20:20:71:539:546:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:it  is  possible  for  the  rest-of-the-world  exports  to  respond  to  that  firm’s  change  in  productivity.
42:1462:103982:20:21:71:539:526:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:First, we note that this would likely bias our coefficient downwards, as a French firm’s productiv-
42:1463:104081:21:20:71:539:505:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:ity increase would induce other exporters to reduce their shipments in their competing markets.
42:1464:104176:20:20:71:539:485:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We can nevertheless address this potential endogeneity directly by dropping any firm that could
42:1465:104271:20:21:71:539:465:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:hypothetically impact rest-of-world exports.  We have experimented with several different market
42:1466:104367:21:20:71:539:444:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:thresholds, and have found that exclusions of those firms either leave those key coefficients on the
42:1467:104467:20:20:71:317:424:11:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f6:trade shock unaffected, or sometimes raises them.44
42:1468:104518:20:21:88:539:404:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:The firms with high export intensity therefore have a response of productivity to trade shocks
42:1469:104612:21:20:71:539:383:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:estimated around 10% (columns 5 and 6 of Table 12).  How should we interpret this number in
42:1470:104703:20:20:71:539:363:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:terms of the impact of our mechanism on the productivity of the French economy as a whole?  As we
42:1471:104800:20:21:71:539:343:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:previously discussed, our firm-level measure of productivity does not correspond directly to physical
42:1472:104901:21:20:71:539:322:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:productivity since we cannot adjust for changes in firm-product prices and qualities.  However, as
42:1473:104999:20:20:71:539:302:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:we pointed out (see equation 18), this firm-level productivity measure can be aggregated up to a
42:1474:105095:20:21:71:539:282:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:sector level measure of physical productivity using the firms’ labor shares.  Side-stepping the issues
42:1475:105197:21:20:71:539:261:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of measurement for the sector price indexPI, we can directly measure the separate contribution of
42:1476:105294:20:20:71:263:241:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the trade shock to sectoral productivity.
42:1477:105335:20:21:88:539:221:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:We start with the coefficient on the trade shock for the high export-intensity quartileβq4for the
42:1478:105432:21:20:72:540:200:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:specifications in first differences.  This gives us the percentage productivity response to a 1% trade
42:1479:105534:20:20:72:540:180:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:shock for the firms in the fourth quartile. We assume that there is no response of productivity for the
42:1480:105637:20:16:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:firms in the remaining 3 quartiles.  We can then use each firm’s measured trade shock to calculate an
42:1481:105738:16:15:80:539:144:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:43Since the trade shock as not been interacted with export intensity, the coefficients for this top quartile represent
42:1482:105856:15:11:72:540:129:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:significantly higher magnitudes than the average coefficients across the whole sample reported in Table 7 (since export
42:1483:105975:11:11:72:540:118:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:intensity is always below 1).  This is also confirmed by a specification with the interacted trade shock restricted to
42:1484:106093:11:7:72:197:107:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:this same top quartile of firms.
42:1485:106125:7:15:80:539:100:6:g_d0_f7:9:g_d0_f8:44For  example,  one  of  our  more  aggressive  thresholds  was  the  exclusion  of  any  firm  with  an  average  product-

44:1542:109544:20:20:71:539:323:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:productivity changes range from an average annual impact of 3.4 % for wearing apparel, to a small
44:1543:109641:20:21:71:539:303:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:negative impact for refined petroleum for instance.  Overall, we find that the aggregate impact of
44:1544:109739:21:20:71:539:282:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the trade shocks is substantial – accounting for a 1.2% average productivity growth rate for the
44:1545:109835:20:20:71:539:262:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:entire French manufacturing sector (working only through the productivity linkages for the firms
44:1546:109931:20:20:71:539:242:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:with the highest export intensities).  This amounts to a 12% productivity gain over our ten year
44:1547:110027:20:21:71:539:222:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:sample period from 1995-2005.  Taking the ratio of the annual 1.2% productivity gain to the 6.2%
44:1548:110123:21:20:71:539:201:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:trade shock change,  we see that the .092 firm-level elasticity obtained in Table 12 is doubled to
44:1549:110221:20:20:71:539:181:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:.193 for aggregate productivity.  This reflects the contribution of labor share reallocations towards
44:1550:110322:20:38:71:266:161:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:firms with relatively higher trade shocks.
44:1551:110364:38:31:71:156:123:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:7  Conclusion
44:1552:110377:31:20:71:539:92:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:This paper uses detailed firm-level data to assess the relevance and magnitude of a new channel
44:1553:110472:20:0:71:539:72:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:of gains from trade:  the productivity gains associated with demand shocks in export markets (via

45:1579:111537:16:36:77:416:347:9:g_d0_f8:9:g_d0_f8:Columns (1) and (2) provide average percentage changes over the 1996-2005 period.
45:1580:111618:36:21:72:540:311:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:the induced effect of demand on the product mix of exporters).  Our theoretical model predicts
45:1581:111712:21:20:72:540:290:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:that  demand  shocks  generate  an  endogenous  increase  in  local  competition  that  induces  firms  to
45:1582:111818:20:20:72:540:270:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:skew their sales towards their better performing products – generating increases in productivity.
45:1583:111915:20:21:72:540:250:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Empirically, our data matches individual export flows by French firms to each country in the world
45:1584:112013:21:20:72:540:229:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:with balance sheet data needed to evaluate the impact of demand shocks on productivity and to
45:1585:112106:20:20:72:540:209:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:control for confounding factors.  The strategy is therefore to look at product mix changes inside the
45:1586:112207:20:21:72:540:189:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:firm, rather than reallocations of market shares across firms.  We can therefore control for many
45:1587:112304:21:20:72:540:168:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:alternative  explanations  that  might  be  correlated  with  foreign  demand  shocks  –  a  strategy  that
45:1588:112411:20:20:72:372:148:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:would not be possible when evaluating the effectsacrossfirms.
45:1589:112472:20:21:88:539:128:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:Our baseline results shows that the elasticity of labor productivity to trade shocks is between
45:1590:112567:21:20:72:540:107:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:5 and 11 %.  This order of magnitude is very robust to controls for short-run investment by the
45:1591:112662:20:0:72:540:87:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:firm,  scale  effects,  and  possibly  correlated  import  shocks.   Our  measured  productivity  effect  for
46:1592:112773:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:single product firms is nil, further highlighting the importance of changes in product mix for multi-
46:1593:112874:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:product firms.  We also show that this productivity response is concentrated within the quartile of
46:1594:112973:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:exporters with the highest export intensities.  Taking into account the weight of those firms in the
46:1595:113073:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:whole economy, we calculate that the average annual increase in French manufacturing productivity
46:1596:113170:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:– in response to growth in world trade – over our 10 year sample (from 1995-2005) is slightly over
46:1597:113268:20:37:72:162:607:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:1 percent per year.
